  • Using Surrogate Indices to Estimate Long-Run Heterogeneous Treatment Effects of Membership…

    tech-at-instacart - Medium:

    Using Surrogate Indices to Estimate Long-Run Heterogeneous Treatment Effects of Membership Incentives

    Authors: Levi Boxell, Changyao Chen, Rustin Partow, Mack Sweeney

    This is the fourth post in a series covering the Economics Team at Instacart and areas we work on. If you missed it, be sure to have a look at our previous post introducing the Economics Team at Instacart as well as two more applied posts: one on using regression discontinuity to optimize authorization buffers and one on using bandits for marketing optimization.

    In this post, we will focus on how we use surrogate indices to estimate long-run heterogeneous treatment effects of our membership incentive portfolio. This project was a collaboration with our colleague from the Growth Data Science team, Mack Sweeney.

    Membership Incentives at Instacart

    At Instacart, we have a paid membership program (Instacart+) that offers various benefits for members, including free delivery on orders of a certain size and access to exclusive partnership offers. To let users explore the benefits of membership, we offer incentives like free trials or discounted annual memberships to certain users.

    What kinds of potential users should we give an incentive to and for how much? These questions are central to the success of this program. Our goal is to give membership discounts that maximize users’ long-term value (LTV) to Instacart, holding constant other business decisions.

    Answering these questions requires addressing two important problems:

    1. Creating a metric that appropriately captures the LTV of a user
    2. Building an algorithm that can learn how to make user-level discount decisions that optimize the above metric

    Below, we focus on how we use surrogates to tackle the first problem. In a later post, we will discuss how we use contextual bandits and meta-learners to address the second problem.

    Surrogate Indices

    A surrogate is a proxy or placeholder for the true (unobserved) outcome of interest. The true outcome is unobserved, either because it hasn’t happened yet or because it has happened but we simply don’t have access to the data.

    In industry, we are typically in the, “hasn’t happened yet” situation where we have lots of contemporaneous data on users, but need predictions of long-term effects.

    For example, an e-commerce site might assume a linear relationship between the total user-level revenue over the month following a membership discount and the LTV of that user. By looking at the historical relationship between month 1 user revenue and LTV, the e-commerce company can estimate a “multiplier” (or coefficient) to map from monthly revenue to LTV:

    LTV = multiplier x month 1 user revenue

    Typically, e-commerce sites want to estimate the incremental LTV (iLTV) of a new incentive, i.e., the effect of the treatment on LTV. The same multiplier can be used for this. Simply estimate the month 1 user revenue treatment effect (e.g., via an A/B test) and multiply:

    iLTV = multiplier x month 1 user revenue treatment effect.

    While this framework is straightforward, a single surrogate variable often has limitations. Suppose there are two growth initiatives. One encourages customers to place bigger orders (increasing monthly revenue while keeping orders fixed). The other encourages customers to order more frequently in exchange for temporary price reductions (reducing short-term revenue but increasing monthly orders). To have any hope of projecting the LTV of the two different initiatives, we need a surrogate that somehow controls for both short-term monthly orders and monthly revenue. To do this, we need a surrogate index!

    A surrogate index is a mapping between multiple observed variables and an unobserved outcome of interest (see Athey et al. 2019 for additional details). Typically, the observed variables are a combination of short-run post-treatment outcomes (such as the number of orders and total revenue over a single month) and pretreatment variables (such as the average revenue per month for a user prior to treatment). In this context, these short-run post-treatment outcomes are surrogates, hence the name surrogate index.

    To create a surrogate index in the standard paradigm, we need a dataset for which we observe the long-term outcome. The dataset can either be an observational dataset or one composed of prior experiments for which long-run outcomes are observable.

    The exact model used for a surrogate index depends on our beliefs about the conditional expectation of the long-term outcome given the surrogates and pretreatment features. That is, we need to estimate:

    E(LTV | surrogates, pretreatment features)

    Suppose we believe the conditional expectation is linear in the surrogates. Then we could use an ordinary least squares (OLS) model, i.e., a weighted combination of surrogates:

    LTV = b_1 x month 1 user revenue + b_2 x month 1 user order count.

    If we instead believe the expectation is likely nonlinear, we can use a more flexible model (e.g., XGBoost) that predicts LTV based on many surrogates and pretreatment features:

    LTV = f(surrogates, pretreatment features).

    There are two key statistical assumptions to keep in mind when constructing a surrogate index:

    1. Surrogacy — the set of surrogates (i.e., short-run outcomes) should cover all causal paths between treatment and the long-run outcome.
    2. Comparability — the mapping (i.e., the conditional expectation) between surrogates and the long-run outcome must be the same in the training data and the future experimental data the surrogate index will be applied to.

    Surrogacy fails in the earlier example where the new incentive impacted order frequency and only one-month revenue was used as a surrogate. Surrogacy is the motivation for the index approach.

    Comparability fails when the regime changes. For example, a surrogate index trained on data from a period in which no incentives existed would not likely provide valid predictions for treatment effects during a period with abundant incentives. Comparability motivates careful choice of the training data to mimic the environment in which the index will ultimately be applied.

    The use of surrogate indices at a tech company is not novel — e.g., see here. However, we faced a unique challenge in that we wanted to compute long-run treatment effects over a period in which we didn’t actually have comparable data. To address this, we developed a parametric projection approach that we can validate over the period for which we do have comparable data and therefore build confidence in our estimate over periods for which we do not have comparable data.

    So keep reading!

    Experiment Library

    Before we get to the modeling, we should talk about the data we use. One’s choice of data to train surrogate indices on is crucial for ensuring that the comparability assumption is (approximately) valid.

    In conjunction with our Data Science team, we built an experiment library that stores data on prior incentive experiments (not just membership incentives) in a unified and centralized manner. Previously, this knowledge and data was distributed across multiple teams.

    We leverage the experiment library in two ways. First, rather than training our surrogate index on purely observational data (e.g., users sampled at completely random points in time), we train it on the records in past experiments to maximize comparability to our future usage for incentive program measurement.

    Parameterizing NPV and Model Training

    At Instacart, we are often interested in optimizing for multi-year net present value (NPV), which is the cumulative variable contribution profit (VCP) discounted back to the present over some investment horizon.

    We have only a small set of users for which we have observed multiple years of VCP. If we restrict ourselves to experiments with fully observable post-treatment data, our available sample is restricted even further. This prevents us from training our surrogate model directly on the long-run NPV.

    However, we feel confident in our sample of users with at least 1 year of observable VCP. To take advantage of this data, we parametrize a VCP trajectory model and train it on 12–18 months of observable post-treatment data in our experiment library.

    With the VCP trajectory model, rather than predicting a single value for NPV, we predict the full sequence of VCP for each post-intervention month.

    A key challenge is enabling our VCP trajectory model to fit non-monotonic trajectories. For example (as shown below via fabricated figures), an IC+ membership incentive may lose VCP over time due to discounted fees, and then generate VCP in the long-run from improved retention and membership renewal dues.

    To capture the non-monotonic trajectory, we flexibly estimate the levels for each of the initial few periods post-treatment and then use a simpler parameterized model to estimate a single long-running post-treatment trend for each user.

    For estimating levels, we use XGBoost to predict each month’s level as a function of (a) pre-treatment features, (b) surrogates, and (c) a feature containing the specific post-treatment month of the prediction. By including the time feature, we allow the model to flexibly learn non-parametric relationships between the outcome and each time period.

    For estimation of the trends subsequent to the first few periods, we specify a growth model of the outcome based on our institutional knowledge. For example, we could specify a logarithmic growth model:

    log(VCP_t) = ɑ * log(VCP_t-1).

    Note that this is governed by a single scalar parameter ɑ which may vary across users. We can then invert the growth model and train an XGBoost model to predict the heterogeneous scalar parameter given log(VCP_t) / log(VCP_t-1), i.e.,

    ɑ = log(VCP_t) / log(VCP_t-1).

    For prediction, we combine the two models. For the initial few periods, we use the levels as predicted by the levels model. For the subsequent periods, we use the growth model with the predicted heterogeneous growth parameter to extrapolate the trajectory of our predictions.

    Lastly, we do not model VCP directly. Rather, we decompose VCP into its subcomponents and model the trajectory separately for each component as outlined above. Specifically, we decompose VCP into the probability a user is an active orderer in a given month (pMAO) and the expected VCP conditional on being MAO. Thus, VCP can be written as:

    VCP = p(MAO) * E(VCP | MAO = 1).

    Then we estimate the semiparametric and parametric trend models separately for each subcomponent.

    Our decisions to predict the full trajectory of VCP, include semi-parametric and parametric trend models, and decompose VCP into subcomponents has several advantages.

    1. It utilizes additional information about the impact of the intervention (i.e., trends, not just levels).
    2. It provides a parametric model for simulating outcomes for out-of-sample periods for which we do not have useful training data, but for which we have institutional knowledge.
    3. It facilitates backtesting across a range of experiments with different durations (See Backtesting Section below).
    4. It facilitates continual evaluation of predictions from live experiments as we observe the true values each month.

    Backtesting

    At this point, we have a semi-parametric, longitudinal model of each customer’s profit over time, for which we’ve done the usual out-of-sample validation on vanilla predictive fit (i.e., predicting a given outcome’s level). But how accurately (and precisely) can our model recover the desired long-run treatment effects? This is where backtesting comes in.

    Because we can accurately estimate long-run treatment effects from older experiments in our experiment library, these provide a natural candidate for backtesting the capability of our model to provide accurate imputations of the long-run treatment effects. Thus, our most important testing procedure consists of comparing the average treatment effects (ATEs) on actual long-term outcomes that we can estimate via our experiment library against the average treatment effects on our surrogate index. Testing for equality of the two sets of treatment effects is equivalent to jointly testing the surrogate index assumptions we made above (although we must keep in mind that the test is limited to the treatments and time-horizons that are covered by the experiment library).

    Medium-run vs. long-run. An important caveat to our approach is that long-term outcomes that are many years into the future factor heavily into decisions based on customer LTV, but we can only backtest based on medium-term outcomes. Thus, there’s a theoretical risk that surrogate indices that perform well at medium-run treatment effect imputation are secretly bad at long-run treatment effect imputation.

    This is the main reason that we employ a semi-parametric modeling strategy. Our model produces a generic time-free estimate of several future intermediate outcomes (retention and profit per retained customer), extrapolates those estimates to different horizons based on a few very low-dimensional parameters, and combines retention and profit per retained customer into a final profit outcome. By imposing this parsimonious structure in the model, we believe we are increasing the correlation between medium-term and long-term accuracy. Furthermore, by visually inspecting the trends in the accuracy of the imputed treatment effects over time, we can make reasonable extrapolations of how accurate it will be in the long-term.

    Pre-treatment dimensions. One of the major applications of our surrogate index is to estimate the efficacy of personalized targeting policies that change the mix of interventions given to different segments of customers. For this kind of application, it’s ideal to have a surrogate index that yields accurate conditional average treatment effect (CATE) imputations, conditional on the type of information that is typically used in targeting applications, and not merely accurate ATE imputations. To test for accurate CATE imputation, we re-run our comparisons between actual and imputed ATEs along a suite of pretreatment customer segments.

    Results. Overall, we were able to demonstrate significant improvements in overall ATE accuracy, even larger improvements in CATE accuracy for individual segments, and more reassuring trends in ATE accuracy, as a result of replacing simple univariate translation methods with a high-dimensional ML-powered surrogate index.

    Overall, our resulting surrogate index exhibited significant improvements over our status-quo frameworks for measuring long-run treatment effects.

    Deploying and Continual Evaluation

    With our models trained, we set up a daily batch job to score all users on their full VCP trajectory path. We use these VCP trajectories to construct experimental metrics for ongoing tests as well as for potential use in our underlying incentive targeting ML models.

    Moreover, as we mentioned before, an advantage of predicting the full trajectory is that we can perform continual evaluation of our model’s performance on live experiments or post-launch of an incentive program as outlined in the image below.

    Wrapping Up

    Membership incentive targeting is just one example of how surrogate indices can be applied. More generally, they are well-suited for measuring the long-term treatment effects of any short-run treatment. See here for discussion on measuring the long-term effects of persistent treatments.

    If you would generally like to learn more about our work, check out the intro to our team or our posts on using regression discontinuity or multi-arm bandits to solve business problems.

    Lastly, stay tuned for a future post on using offline contextual bandits for membership incentive targeting! You can follow tech-at-instacart to be notified when it is published.

    Using Surrogate Indices to Estimate Long-Run Heterogeneous Treatment Effects of Membership… was originally published in tech-at-instacart on Medium, where people are continuing the conversation by highlighting and responding to this story.

  • Data Science Spotlight: Cracking the SQL Interview at Instacart (LLM Edition)

    tech-at-instacart - Medium:

    By: Anahita Tafvizi, Michael Curran, Monta Shen

    Data Scientists at Instacart require a unique combination of skills to be successful on the job. They need to have a combination of business acumen, analytical, communication and technical skills. The Data Science interview loop tests the candidate for these skills, beginning with the Technical Screen — including a SQL interview where candidates show that they can translate business questions into code that retrieves the correct data from databases. This set of questions generally provides the candidate with a set of schemas and common business questions where they then leverage SQL to translate data into insights.

    Here’s an example of such a question:

    Using these two tables, ascertain:

    1. What are average ratings by day? Include only users with 5+ orders.
    2. Is there a relationship between order number and average ratings?

    LLM Cracks the Interview

    Before the rise in popularity of LLMs for coding use cases, Data Scientists would have to write code manually to retrieve data through databases and manipulate the data to achieve the desired insight. Now that LLMs are widely accessible, Data Scientists are able to write and edit code through natural language, saving significant amounts of time and effort.

    A more efficient way to ask and answer the above interview question would be to simply ask LLMs. An example prompt would include the schemas above, the questions and the task.

    Here are the schemas:
    <<INSERT SCHEMAS>>
    Here are the questions:
    << INSERT QUESTIONS>>
    Here is the task:
    Write Snowflake SQL to answer the above questions.

    Here’s a truncated depiction of what might happen when we use this prompt in Ava, Instacart’s internal AI assistant powered by OpenAI and other models. Ava is able to write all the necessary SQL to answer the interview questions.

    Through a quick test of this prompt via a few popular LLMs (e.g. GPT-4o, Snowflake Arctic and Llama 3–70B), each is able to do these tasks correctly.

    Rethinking the SQL Interview at Instacart

    Having candidates write live SQL to test their ability to code is both ineffective and a poor representation of on-the-job workflows. This usually leads to questions needing to be extremely simple in order to fit a time constraint and unfairly penalizes candidates if they do not write SQL daily. Moreover, interviews that can be solved easily through a simple prompt and relevant context are not effective ways to test candidates–especially considering Instacart Data Scientists will be expected to leverage AI in their workflows.

    Given this evolution, we’re making changes to our SQL interview process to orient more around AI-forward workflows that have become best practice on our team. Now, as part of their SQL interview, Instacart Data Science candidates may be asked to:

    • Translate an insight into a prompt for a SQL query — this tests a candidate’s ability to prompt engineer and translate a business question into an actionable data pull.
    • Explain and debug a sample SQL query — this tests a candidate’s ability to understand and fix LLM-generated SQL outputs.
    • Identify ways to make a sample SQL query more efficient — this tests a candidate’s deep understanding of SQL, both in writing and processing efficiently.

    This reimagined SQL interview, combined with our other technical and non-technical interviews (e.g. product sense, statistics, cross-functional partnership, analytics), will give the team a better understanding of candidate skills and allow us to continue to up-level the Data Science team at Instacart.

    For more information on the interview process and to see our open Data Science roles, please visit Instacart’s Careers Page.

    Data Science Spotlight: Cracking the SQL Interview at Instacart (LLM Edition) was originally published in tech-at-instacart on Medium, where people are continuing the conversation by highlighting and responding to this story.

    Introduction

    Earlier in 2024, the iOS teams at Thumbtack were working to migrate to Xcode 15.3 to take advantage of all of the new features Apple’s latest IDE offers (such as Swift 5

  • Enhancing FoodStorm with AI Image Generation

    tech-at-instacart - Medium:

    If you’re a frequent Instacart customer, you’ve probably already experienced some of our innovative AI-powered features, like Ask Instacart, which delivers personalized shopping recommendations through natural language processing. What’s less known is that Instacart also equips grocers with sophisticated digital tools to streamline their operations. And yes, AI can significantly enhance these tools as well.

    Take Instacart’s Order Management System (OMS), FoodStorm, for instance. FoodStorm allows grocers to offer customizable products: think freshly made sandwiches, sliced deli meats, and personalized cakes — not to mention a full Thanksgiving dinner complete with all the trimmings. These items are far more complex than your standard “center aisle” assortment because of their customizable nature; for example, choosing from various sandwich fillings or cake decorations.

    So how can AI play a part here? An ongoing bugbear our grocers face is sourcing images for every possible product configuration. High quality food photography is crucial for online shopping, elevating the consumer’s experience. This is where Generative AI comes in — it can produce stellar images of virtually any food product, offering a feasible solution for showcasing diverse food options.

    Some of you might be gasping at the thought of AI-generated images. It’s essential to clarify that nothing can replace the authenticity of professional photographs showing the real product. However, for visualizing various fillings, toppings and sides, Generative AI can be a huge help. Our tests show that AI excels at creating these kinds of images — think perfectly diced onions, finely shredded lettuce, or just the right amount of strawberry jam.

    A selection of images created by Google Imagen. Imagen also does a great job of creating images “on a white background”, which is a challenge sometimes for other AI tools.

    Integrating AI into FoodStorm began with leveraging Instacart’s in-house Content AI team. We were lucky to have a dedicated text-to-image service, called Pixel, already operational. Pixel provides easy access to a range of different AI models, including Google Imagen, and has controls around acceptable use of prompts to generate images. It was a dream to integrate with and saved us considerable time.

    However, the main challenge was to create the user interface design to allow retailers to create AI images themselves. Our initial design allowed for one-click AI image generation for any sandwich fillings, impressively demonstrated in our early tests and generated much internal excitement:

    Our initial AI image generation spike was coded directly into FoodStorm OMS.

    But the design had flaws. Simply using the filling name as the prompt, like “cheese,” was too vague — what type of cheese, and sliced or grated? These nuances were missing, leading to suboptimal images.

    To address this, we wireframed a new design where users can fine-tune their prompts, specifying details like, “sliced monterey jack cheese on a white background.” Users can also preview multiple image variations to choose the most appropriate result:

    We then landed on a third iteration that also provides a larger preview of the selected image. This allows users to take a close look at the details of the image, as occasionally you’ll get artifacts that may be slightly off. As we all know, AI doesn’t always nail it on the first try.

    Find the perfect grated cheese, checking closely for errors.

    An example of the AI model getting it wrong — is that an Australian Bilby nibbling those raspberries?! Arguably, sales might go up with an image as cute as this.

    Aside from product images, FoodStorm uses Generative AI to create hero images, promotional content, category thumbnails and more. The new Create AI Image dialog box builds on our existing image upload component — so anywhere that users could previously upload an image, they could now instantly create an AI image. This feature alone has enabled us to produce stunning promotional visuals effortlessly.

    Generative AI is not just limited to basic ingredients!

    A quick and easy promotional banner created using AI.

    The integration process also involved compressing and scaling the images post-generation to optimize them for web use, and serving them efficiently through our content delivery network. From a legal standpoint, users must also agree to relevant terms of use before using this feature. Now, AI-generated images can be published across the Instacart Platform, including the Instacart App, Storefront & Storefront Pro and FoodStorm Kiosk for in-store ordering.

    AI represents an exciting new frontier, and it’s essential to balance its use with genuine photography and other creative channels. When used in the right way, and with quality controls in place, AI can be a significant time saver and dynamic creative tool. We’re eager to see how our grocery retailers embrace FoodStorm’s AI capabilities.

    If you’re interested in what FoodStorm can do for your grocery prepared foods business, contact us today: https://www.foodstorm.com/book-demo.

  • Bandits for Marketing Optimization

    tech-at-instacart - Medium:

    Toban Wiebe, Fang Guo, Rustin Partow, and Tilman Drerup

    This is the third post in a series covering the Economics Team at Instacart and the areas we work on. If you missed them, be sure to have a look at our previous post introducing the Economics Team at Instacart and our post highlighting how we used regression discontinuity designs to optimize authorization buffers.

    In this post, we venture into the realm of paid marketing, an area rich with challenging causal inference problems. Our post presents a framework that we have recently developed at Instacart to optimize the efficiency of our paid marketing budgets via adaptive experimentation. While we explicitly focus on paid marketing in this post, the topic of adaptive experimentation is an exciting one that we are particularly passionate about and we will release future blog posts to dive deeper into several impactful applications.

    Problem: Efficient Allocation of Marketing Budgets

    Companies like Instacart deploy vast paid marketing budgets to attract and retain customers. When deciding how much to spend, such companies typically try to maximize an objective function that relates the return generated through a particular marketing campaign to its cost:

    Objective := Return — Cost

    The problem for marketers boils down to understanding how a given marketing action (e.g., setting a target cost per action or CPA) relates to this objective. The plot below presents one such “performance curve”, relating the marketing action to the company’s objective. Once we have identified this curve, it becomes straightforward to identify the action that maximizes the objective.

    The plot above provides an example of a performance curve for a hypothetical marketing campaign (i.e., simulated data). While the x-axis indicates the chosen marketing action (e.g., the level of target CPA), the y-axis shows the value of the objective at this point. The curve presents our current best estimate of the relationship between the two.

    Herein lies the problem: Getting a believable estimate of such performance curves is extremely challenging. In reality, the actions taken by the company in the past are anything but random. As a result, all the observational data from past marketing actions is deeply confounded. For example, marketers will often lean into specific events or holidays in anticipation of an increase in customer interest. Similarly, competitors may decide to spend more, which increases the cost-per-click and total spend, but reduces performance in terms of achieved returns. In these cases, the correlations in the observational data would result in getting the sign of the relationship wrong! Evidently, such observational curve estimates are unreliable to guide decision-making.

    Solution: Adaptive Experimentation

    So how do we solve this? By using adaptive experimentation!

    At Instacart, we have developed an adaptive experimentation system that intelligently introduces random perturbations into our marketing actions. These random perturbations generate the exogenous variation needed to credibly identify and map out performance curves. Over time, this allows us to gradually move from more exploration (to accurately learn the curves) to exploitation (to maximize our returns).

    Before going into more detail, it’s worth taking a step back to briefly describe what adaptive experimentation is all about. We can think of an adaptive experiment as an intermediate between running a fully randomized experiment and no experiment at all. With a fully randomized experiment, we allocate a set number of observations to each treatment arm, obtaining maximal exploration. However, we also incur inefficiencies because we keep the worse performing arms active. By contrast, with no experiment, we maximally exploit what we believe to be the best arm, but we also incur great risk of being wrong as we do not explore other arms at all. Adaptive experiments strike a balance between exploration and exploitation by randomizing across arms, but with an adaptive bias toward better performing arms.

    Under the Hood

    Let’s now get back to the problem of optimizing our marketing spend. At a high level, our adaptive experimentation system consists of two steps that repeat in a continuous loop:

    1. Step N: Modeling Performance Curves. At a regular cadence, the system updates its estimates of the performance curves of all the marketing campaigns it controls. These updates incorporate all the latest observations. Importantly, they incorporate the action propensities that were generated by the second step to ensure valid causal inference (more on that below).
    2. Step N + 1: Choice of the Next Action. Once the system has updated its estimates of the performance curves, an explore-exploit algorithm determines the appropriate next action for each campaign. This algorithm intelligently trades off the value of further exploration (or more randomization) against the cost of choosing an action that may not be optimal.

    Let’s unpack both steps in more detail.

    Modeling Performance Curves

    Our system frequently updates its estimates of performance curves, incorporating the latest data on historical actions and outcomes. The key component of our causal inference strategy is inverse-propensity-weighted (IPW) regression. This is a common causal inference technique which re-weights the dataset to correct for selection bias, in this case stemming from favoring actions that are believed to be optimal. For example, data-points from actions that are less likely to be chosen are given larger weight, because such observations are more informative. We provide more details on this process in the next section.

    We currently estimate curves using a Bayesian parametric model, parameterized to enforce several constraints that prevent implausible curve fits and to embed structural knowledge about the outcomes. For example, we know that the performance curve must start from zero when spend is zero. We have found that this approach works quite well in practice, though we are actively exploring alternatives (e.g., Gaussian Process regression, a Bayesian nonparametric model).

    We use a functional form based on the Beta distribution’s pdf, shown here. With two parameters, it is quite flexible and can represent a wide variety of hump-shaped curves, including asymmetric ones.

    Choice of the Next Action

    To strike a balance between exploration and exploitation, our model does not simply output the best fitting curve — it produces a Bayesian posterior distribution over parameter vectors representing different possible curves. This distribution reflects the probability of each parameter vector being the true representation of the performance curve given the observed historical data.

    With this posterior distribution over performance curves in hand, we then leverage the Thompson Sampling algorithm to make intelligent tradeoffs between exploration and exploitation. If the observed data is noisy and we are less certain about our estimate of the true performance curve, the algorithm will nudge us to do more exploration. Gradually, as the model builds more confidence through more and better data, it shifts toward more exploitation. This entire explore-exploit process is done in a fully automated way. In practice, the Thompson Sampling algorithm’s implementation is astonishingly simple:

    1. Draw a parameter vector from the model’s posterior distribution.
    2. Assume that these parameters specify the true curve and take the optimal action for that curve.

    System in Action

    The graph below provides an illustration of the system running on two rounds of simulated data. In the first round, the performance curve is fitted to all the available data. Next, an action is chosen based on the Thomson sampling algorithms. As new data arrives in round 2, the system repeats this process, first updating its model of the performance curve and then choosing a new action via Thompson Sampling.

    Illustration of system behavior using simulated data.

    Wrapping Up

    Adaptive experimentation systems are proving exceedingly useful at Instacart. They strike an appealing balance between competing objectives and are based on solid and intuitive theoretical foundations. They do, however, also come with additional technical challenges that require a rigorous engineering solution. We have found that the cost associated with the latter is more than worth it. In controlled A/B tests of the system described above, for example, we have seen large improvements in marketing efficiency relative to our existing processes.

    If you would generally like to learn more about our work, check out the intro to our team and stay tuned for more posts about our work. You can follow tech-at-instacart to be notified as they are published.

    Bandits for Marketing Optimization was originally published in tech-at-instacart on Medium, where people are continuing the conversation by highlighting and responding to this story.

  • Unlocking 66% Faster Experiments with Covariate Adjustment: Power Analysis for Clustered Data…

    tech-at-instacart - Medium:

    Unlocking 66% Faster Experiments with Covariate Adjustment: Power Analysis for Clustered Data, Ratio Metrics, and OLS Regression

    Tim Hesterberg and Benjamin Knight

    We describe how to calculate standard errors for A/B tests that include clustered data, ratio metrics, and/or covariate adjustment. We may do this for power analysis/sample size calculations prior to running an experiment using historical data, or after an experiment for hypothesis testing and confidence intervals. The different applications have a common framework, using the sample variance of certain residuals. The framework is compatible with modular software, can be plugged into standard tools, doesn’t require computing covariance matrices, and is numerically stable. Using this approach we estimate that covariate adjustment gives a median 66% variance reduction for a key metric, reducing experiment run time by 66%. Readers who prefer precise notation can find a version of this article on arXiv.¹

    Power Analysis — Where Standard Tools Fall Short

    When running A/B tests (randomized controlled trials), time is money. The faster we can run experiments, the faster we can ship promising treatments. We might put a lot of effort into variance-reduction techniques to obtain more accurate answers, but if power analysis/sample size planning tools don’t reflect that then the experiments we design will run longer than necessary. Most off-the-shelf power analysis tools handle the simple case where analysis uses 𝑡-tests for independent observations, but not clustered data, ratio metrics, or variance-reduction methods. In this article we present a framework for conducting power analysis for A/B tests that can support any combination of the following applications:

    Clustered Data: If we are interested in testing a feature that improves the customer experience, then the most intuitive unit of randomization for an A/B test is the customer. However, customers may place multiple orders. If the metric of interest is at the level of the order (e.g. mean order size) then we need to take this clustering into account when calculating standard errors.

    Ratio Metrics: Some metrics are a ratio between two random quantities, e.g. ‘Revenue Share from Electronics’ = (revenue from electronics)/(total revenue). Standard errors depend on the variances of the numerator, denominator, and their correlation.

    Covariate Adjustment: While random assignment makes experiment arms balanced on average, random imbalances do occur. We can reduce the variance of estimates by correcting for this covariate imbalance using regression. Standard errors should reflect this improvement.

    These applications reduce to four basic cases, combinations of simple means or ratio metrics (including clustered data), with or without covariate adjustment. In all cases we obtain standard errors using the sample standard deviations of certain residuals. We begin with a review of power analysis for unadjusted means in the next section, and consider the other cases in the sections on Ratio Metrics and Clustered Data, Covariate Adjustment, and Covariate Adjustment for Ratio Metrics. The penultimate section (Applications) includes a summary of the standard errors and residuals in Table 1, as well as a real-world example and a meta-analysis from Instacart.

    Conventional Power Analysis: A Refresher

    Power analysis (or sample size planning) involves relationships between four parameters of interest:

    1. sample size (n) representing the number of units selected for experimental assignment,
    2. false positive rate (type I error rate) α,
    3. power (1−β, where β is the type II error rate), the probability of detecting differences of a given magnitude, and
    4. minimum detectable effect (MDE) — the change in the response variable that is detectable with the given power.

    For simplicity we focus on two-arm experiments (“control” and “treatment” arms, denoted using C and T) and focus on power and sample size estimates for a single metric. Let’s define the following terms:

    We will focus on one- sided tests because the vast majority of experiments at Instacart are run for the purpose of testing whether a treatment causes a metric to improve. We assume that sample sizes are large enough that both estimates and their corresponding t-statistics are approximately normally distributed.

    The four parameters are related by the equation:

    where zα and zβ are the Normal quantiles corresponding to the type I and II error rates respectively, and SE ∆ depends on n.

    Power Analysis for a Difference of Means

    For the simple case of a difference in means assuming equal variances, no clustering, and equal sample sizes

    where sʸ is the standard deviation of the response variable. Then given the standard deviation sʸ and any three of MDE, n, α, or β we can calculate the fourth. That factor of 2 arises in a two-armed experiment. Suppose that there are nᶜ and nᵗ observations in the ‘control’ and ‘treatment’ arms, with sample standard deviations S ᶜ and S ᵗ , then

    But when planning an experiment we don’t have those sample standard deviations or the actual sample sizes; instead we typically estimate both sample standard deviations using a single value sʸ estimated from historical data, and specify what fraction of observations ψ will be allocated to the treatment group; then the above equation reduces to

    In the special case that ψ = 50%, then

    More generally, Figure 1 shows how standard errors depend on ψ. The
    minimum scaling factor is 2 at 50%, and is slightly larger for values near 50%, but increases dramatically when the fraction approaches 0 or 1.

    Also note that p-values may be inaccurate if metrics are skewed and the split is not 50–50. The old “n ≥ 30” rule for the Central Limit Theorem is badly wrong for skewed data. p-values from a one-sample t test are not reasonably accurate until n > 5,000 for an exponential population² or n ≥ 125,000 for some important skewed metrics at Instacart. Two-sample tests with a 50–50 split are better because the skewness cancels out for the estimated values of Ȳ ᵗ − Ȳ ᶜ.

    To recap, for an experiment with a 50–50 split

    the sample size necessary to achieve a specified MDE is

    For splits other than 50–50, substitute √(1 / (1−ψ) + 1 / ψ) for 2, but take care to check that skewness does not invalidate normal approximations.

    Generalizing Beyond Difference of Means

    It turns out that the above equations almost work for clustered data, ratio metrics, and covariate adjustment applications — we just need to replace the value sʸ with other quantities that are based on residual standard deviations. Our broad strategy for deriving these values is shown in Figure 2.

    To correctly estimate standard errors we need to account for two factors. First, when data are clustered there is intra-cluster correlation (the top portion of the Venn diagram); ignoring this typically results in standard errors that are too small, causing inflated false positive rates and too-short confidence intervals. Second, controlling for random imbalances in covariates between arms reduces the variability of estimates; ignoring this results in too-large standard errors. Finally, these factors may occur together. In subsequent sections we describe how to estimate standard errors in these cases, using ratio estimates and residual standard deviations.

    Ratio Metrics and Clustered Data

    In this section we discuss how ratio metrics arise, either due to clustering or natural ratio metrics, and derive standard errors. Our first challenge is clustered data. For example, consider estimating average order size (in dollars). We call this GMV per order (Gross Merchandise Value). Calculating the metric is straightforward, as the total value of items ordered divided by the number of orders. Calculating the standard error is not. We must account for correlations within clusters (for example, orders created by the same customer will tend to be of similar sizes).

    We begin by aggregating the data by cluster to obtain two values for each customer: Yⁱ = total value of items ordered by customer i, and nⁱ = number of orders by customer i. Then the metric is a ratio of ∑ Yⁱ / ∑ nⁱ , or equivalently the ratio of two sample means Y-bar / n-bar. This simplifies the problem in one way — we now have independent observations — but complicates it in others. Instead of a sample mean, we have a ratio of two sample means, and the numerator and denominator are dependent.

    Other metrics represent naturally occurring ratios, even without clustering. For example, some retailers have their own in-store workers pick some orders, then Instacart shoppers deliver them to customers. The fraction of GMV picked by Instacart shoppers is a ratio: GMV picked by Instacart shoppers / total GMV. Clustering may also occur with such natural ratio metrics, e.g. clustering from the order level to shopper or store level.

    We use the following notation to handle ratio metrics, with or without clus- tering. Y corresponds to the metric of interest, or numerator of a ratio. Where there is clustering, we let

    • Yij = Cluster i, Observation j (e.g. customer i, order j)
    • Yi = Σj Yij = Sum for Cluster i

    W corresponds to the denominator, to a cluster size or count,

    • Wi = Σj Wij or ni

    The individual or cluster ratio is

    • Vi = Cluster-level average (or ratio): Yi / Wi

    We estimate the metric or ratio of interest as:

    While it might be natural to think of these metrics as weighted averages, i.e. ∑ Wᵢ Yᵢ / ∑ Wᵢ, doing so makes calculating standard errors tricky (see the appendix in the arXiv version). Instead we estimate standard errors for ratio metrics using the delta method.

    Standard Errors Using the Delta Method and Residuals

    We turn now to calculating standard errors for ratio metrics, whether due to clustering or not. We use the delta method. We find a linear approximation to the ratio, based on a first-order bivariate Taylor series of the function f(W̅, Y̅) = Y̅ / W̅ about (μw, μy),

    where μʸ and μᵂ are the population means for the response variable per cluster and number of observations per cluster respectively. The estimate Θ, is approximately true value of the ratio plus the mean residual divided by the true mean denominator. We visualize this in Figure 3.

    and covariances. We prefer not to do this. Thinking of the variance in terms
    of the variance of residuals is easier to understand, particularly as we con-
    sider covariate adjustment below. Furthermore, that expansion can result in numerically-unstable estimates, including negative variances.

    Covariate Adjustment

    In a randomized controlled trial the assignment of subjects to arms is fair on average, but in any trial there may be imbalances. For example, if the outcome of interest is customer spend, one arm might have more customers with high spend in the month before the experiment starts. We can improve estimates of the experimental effect by correcting for such imbalances. This is covariate adjustment (or CUPED, ANCOVA, controls, control variates, regression adjustment, etc.).

    Figure 4 shows how this works for the case of one covariate (one predictor),
    using linear regression. The mean Y is clearly larger for the treatment group than for the control group. However, that is not solely due to the treatment; the treatment group also has larger X values than the control group, which inflates Y̅ among the treatment group, and depresses Y̅ within the control group. We correct for the imbalance using the predictions at the common mean x̅.

    For multiple regression with 𝑝 predictors we fit separate regression models to the control and treatment data, both of the form

    Then revised estimates for each arm are:

    where x̅ⱼis the common mean of the jth predictor.

    Fitting separate models is equivalent to fitting a single model that includes
    interactions of the treatment variable with all predictors. We could fit a single model that excludes some interactions; this corresponds to fitting separate models but with the constraint that

    for some values of j (and using the same prediction formulas).

    These estimates are a special case of the general rule:

    These averages are over all observations, regardless of which arm i was assigned to; in other words, we estimate what the mean responses would be, if both arms had the same distribution of x values.

    Standard Errors using Residuals

    To calculate the standard errors for covariate adjusted estimates we begin with the residuals. For each group (C and T separately), the residual standard deviation is

    Then the standard error is

    We are intentionally excluding a term from this standard error. Consider the control arm, and let σ²c be the variance of residuals relative to the true regression line/plane. The prediction at x̅c is Y̅c, which has variance σ²c/n, which we estimate using the standard deviation of the residuals from the control arm divided by the number of units of assignment (s²ᵣc / n). The missing term is the extra variance for predictions at other points, in particular at x̅. But in randomized trials with large samples, x̅ is typically close to the group mean x̅c, and the additional variance is negligible.

    Similarly, we are not using heteroskedasticity-consistent (HC) calculations for standard errors or covariance matrices. HC methods would have a negligible impact on variances for predictions at or near x̅.

    In fact, our approach avoids the need to ever estimate covariance matrices for the coefficients. This makes it practical to use covariates with a large number of levels, e.g. customers, using fitting methods that do not produce covariance matrices.

    Covariate Adjustment for Ratio Metrics

    To apply covariate adjustment to ratio metrics, we use regression adjustments independently for the numerator Y and denominator W, obtaining

    The covariate-adjusted ratio estimates are

    We use the delta method to obtain linear approximations for these estimates. Recall that standard errors for ratio metrics and covariate-adjusted non-ratio metrics both involve residuals; the standard errors here involve “double residuals” that combine elements of both residuals. For each arm, let

    These double residuals are like the ratio method residuals

    but with regression residuals yi − ŷi in place of yi, and regression residuals wi − ŵi in place of wi. We calculate the residual variance

    Applications

    Here we review the methodology described above, then consider an example and a meta-analysis. We began with a review of conventional power analysis methods for unadjusted differences of means, then described extensions for (1) clustered data using ratios of means, (2) ratios of means using the delta method, and (3) covariate adjustment for both means and ratios of means using regression. The standard errors of estimates ultimately depend on the sample standard deviations sᵣ of certain residuals, plus division by the estimated denominator mean (w̅ or μ̂ ʷ) for ratio estimates. This is summarized in Table 1 (below).

    We could use common power analysis tools by plugging in sʳ / w̅, or sʳ , or sʳ in place of sʸ. We can incorporate those individual-arm standard errors into standard errors for the difference between arms, e.g. for an adjusted ratio metric

    From there we can calculate the minimum detectable effect

    sample size

    or power

    Let’s walk through an application using real-world data. Instacart has store planogram data for some retailers — detailed descriptions of the exact location of a given product including the aisle number, shelf number, etc. Providing shoppers with this data could speed up their work and increase the proportion of items they find. This should not only make shoppers’ picking experience easier, but it should have the added benefit of letting shoppers work through their orders more quickly, increasing the average value of delivered orders as measured by GTV-per-order.

    To test this, consider an A/B test randomized at the shopper level, with α = 0.05 and a sample size of 10 million orders (or approximately half a million shoppers). We estimate the variance of the average delivery value from historical data and specify an MDE of $0.05 per order. Using standard t-tests would give a severely under-powered experiment, with power of 15% (see Figure 5).

    We can do better using covariate adjustment. The dollar value of the delivered order Y is highly correlated with the dollar value of the order the customer placed (see models 3 and 4 in Table 2). The number of items in the order W is highly correlated with the sum of estimated probabilities of being in stock (models 2 and 4). Using either of these predictors alone has minimal value for covariate adjustment for the ratio of interest, but using them together gives an R² of 0.927 and improves power to over 92%.

    We see how covariate adjustment can increase statistical power, but what about our original mandate — to ship promising treatments as quickly as possible? To explore the impact of covariate adjustment on experiment run times, we conducted a meta-analysis of 3,563 A/B tests comprised of 4,642 individual experiment arms. The response variable for these experiments is Gross Transaction Value (GTV) per customer (this is different from GMV-per-order in Table 2). Instacart adjusts for the following covariates: customer GTV measured during the 60-day pre-assignment period, the customer’s lifetime value (LTV) as estimated from a machine learning model, and the number of days elapsed since experimental assignment.

    Holding statistical power, alpha, and the MDE constant across the covariate adjusted versus non-covariate adjusted versions of these hypothesis tests, we see that the median experiment run time for unadjusted tests is approximately 39 days. In contrast, the median run time using covariate adjustment is 13 days. As a thought exercise, if we were to apply the 26 days of run time saved to all 3,563 experiments, then the total time savings would amount to 253 years. In a world where time, is in fact, money, then the value proposition of covariate adjustment is evident.

    Conclusion

    Using regression in the context of a randomized controlled trial provides a straightforward way to perform covariate adjustment. We must take care not to include covariates that are affected by the treatment, which would bias the results. Nevertheless, covariate adjustment is a powerful tool in our toolkit whenever statistical power is at a premium.

    Unfortunately, most off-the-shelf power analysis tools do not support covariate adjustment. These same tools often fail when presented with clustered data and/or ratio metrics. The approach described above provides a way to conduct power analysis in these cases, without the need for complex simulations.

    We would like to thank Mack Sweeney and Michael Curran for their helpful comments and feedback.

  • Optimizing at the Edge:  Using Regression Discontinuity Designs to Power Decision-Making

    tech-at-instacart - Medium:

    Optimizing at the Edge:
    Using Regression Discontinuity Designs to Power Decision-Making

    Levi Boxell, Robert Fletcher, and Tilman Drerup

    In our previous post, we introduced the Economics Team at Instacart and talked about our unique team structure and the various problems we work on. In a series of follow-up posts, we will take a closer look at how we deploy econometric and machine learning tools to tackle specific business problems.

    In the first post of this series, we will focus on regression discontinuity designs, a powerful econometric technique that can be used to learn causal effects from observational data. We also provide a simple framework for how these estimates can be used to make business-relevant trade-off decisions. We then take this framework to one of the problems we recently worked on and show how we have used it to revise authorization buffers.

    Framework: Regression Discontinuity Designs & Trade-Off Optimization

    Regression Discontinuity Designs

    At Instacart, we frequently rely on a classic quasi-experimental method to answer business questions: regression discontinuity designs. In a regression discontinuity design, we rely on natural break points in a system to causally estimate an effect of interest.

    To gain intuition for the concept, consider an ecommerce site that offers customers different service options based on their time of arrival on the site. For example, imagine the site decided to offer every customer arriving before noon a 2-day delivery window, whereas every customer who arrives right after noon is offered a 3-day window. A customer’s arrival time on the site is of course not random, so we cannot quite learn the causal effect of the change in the delivery window on all customers.

    However, there is an interesting quasi-experiment hidden in this data. Arguably, what is random is whether a customer arrives just before or after noon. Imagine now that we plotted a customer’s likelihood of checking out against the time of arrival relative to the noon cutoff and saw something like the following, where the x-axis shows the time of arrival relative to noon and the y-axis shows the average checkout propensity:

    Right around noon, we see a sizable drop in checkout propensity. At this time, however, the only thing that arguably changes is that we stop showing customers the faster delivery options.

    This drop thus presents us with a local average treatment effect (LATE) for the impact of the expanded delivery window. This effect is local only since it’s only valid for the customers just to the left and to the right of the cutoff. Or, put differently, it presents an estimate of the treatment effect among the specific set of population affected by the threshold. LATEs are valuable pieces of information that can point us in the correct direction, but they can not provide the average treatment effect (ATE) across the entire user population from a change in policy.

    Trading Off Business Objectives

    Regression discontinuity frameworks provide a powerful tool to examine various tradeoffs between metrics around the threshold. Imagine, for instance, the ecommerce site from the example above wanted to evaluate whether it should adjust the delivery threshold to later or earlier in the day. In such a trade-off, decisions would likely be based on a comparison of the incremental value of moving the thresholds and the associated cost. Specifically, moving the threshold to later in the day may generate a number of incremental orders but also put a strain on delivery systems as more orders have to be fulfilled in less time.

    Here is what the site could do to evaluate such a trade-off. Let’s say the site only cares about the number of checkouts and the cost of fulfillment per order. It’s common practice to have a guardrail informed by the long-term value of an incremental order to make such tradeoff decisions. This generates a decision framework that takes the form of:

    Incremental orders — guardrail * (incremental fulfillment cost) > 0.

    Given this framework, the site can simply plug-in the LATE estimates for orders and fulfillment cost respectively to determine whether to expand or contract eligibility for the shorter delivery window.

    Of course, the site would have to ensure that the assumptions underlying this estimation remain valid in the process. To further finetune the threshold, the platform could run an A/B test (or better yet a Multi-Armed Bandit — more on that in a future post!) to evaluate a series of potential thresholds, repeating the above exercise for each of them and charting out the entire trade-off curve.

    Now let’s turn to an example of how we used this line of thinking to optimize Instacart’s Authorization Buffers.

    Application: Authorization Buffer Optimization

    Discontinuous Authorization Buffers at Instacart

    When customers shop on Instacart, we face a challenge: How large of a hold should we put on a customer’s credit card? When a customer clicks “Place Order,” we know the initial cost of the items in the customer’s cart as well as the associated fees, taxes, and tips. However, customers can still make changes or add items after the order has been placed. Shopper-initiated replacements for missing items can also change the final charge amounts.

    To enable such post-checkout alterations, we place a so-called authorization hold based on the initial cart total plus an additional “buffer” amount. The scheme we use to determine such buffers needs to trade off competing effects: While higher authorization holds evidently reduce the risk of potential unpaid amounts, they also create a confusing customer experience, even if customers are only charged the actual amount of their final order upon the order’s completion.

    How can we determine the optimal authorization buffer to add? Here’s where a natural experiment in Instacart’s buffer policy comes in.

    To illustrate, we will turn to a hypothetical version of the authorization buffer scheme that resembles the one that was deployed at Instacart at some point in the past. In this scheme, buffers were allocated as follows:

    1. First, add a buffer of 10% to the order total, and
    2. Second, round the resulting total up to the next $5.

    The second step was added to avoid odd-looking authorizations to the customer. Notice how the second step in the scheme creates a discontinuous effect. Every time step 1 results in an amount that just exceeds a multiple of five, the total authorization amount jumps by $5! As a result, two orders can generate a $5 difference in the authorization hold even if their actual order amounts are only a single cent apart.

    This discontinuous jump gives us everything we need to estimate a local average treatment effect of the higher buffer amount on the business metrics we care about. Of course, the crucial assumption for a causal interpretation is that users just below the $5 multiple and users just above are the same on average. For example, users who place $100 orders should not be doing so because they know that a $100.01 order would trigger a $5 larger authorization buffer. In our setting, we believe this is a safe assumption.

    Local Average Treatment Effect Estimation

    When applying this method to our data, we see something like the following graph (which is for illustrative purposes only). On the x-axis, the graph shows the difference in cents between the customer’s order total and the threshold. The y-axis shows the rate of card declines. At the discontinuity, where the rounding suddenly jumps up to the next $5, we see the users on the right having significantly higher card decline rates than users on the left.

    *The plot is for illustrative purposes and does not reflect actual treatment effects or levels in the data.

    We can of course repeat this analysis with other outcomes we may care about, such as unpaid amounts or re-authorization rates. Combining these estimates with a tradeoff framework helps inform us on whether we should be more or less aggressive in our authorization buffers.

    Authorization Buffers: Navigating the Payment Predicament

    Following the initial analysis, we developed a revised policy based on the estimated effects. To validate our proposed policy and the ATE, however, we still needed to run an A/B experiment. As part of a series of experiments intended to improve the payment experience for our customers, we ran an experiment where we significantly reduced the amount of upward rounding in the initial authorization buffer. The results validated the insights from our regression discontinuity analysis and generated a significant increase in order volume on Instacart through a reduction in the number of card declines!

    By reducing frictions associated with the initial authorization buffer amounts, we were able to generate a win-win for both customers and Instacart, on average. However, within the average treatment effects may lie important heterogeneity. More broadly, our series of experiments pointed to the realization that adjusting the initial authorization buffer is just the start.

    For some users, the initial authorization is a burdensome friction that needs to be reduced. However, reducing the initial authorization amount has the undesired consequence of increasing the likelihood that a second authorization is needed. For many customers, the subsequent authorization attempts may be a confusing and ultimately poor order experience. Our experiments in this domain are building towards the vision of an authorization and payment platform that can tailor the initial and subsequent authorization rulesets based on current order context and past customer interactions. Our rounding experiment was a stepping stone in this direction.

    Wrapping Up

    Applying regression discontinuity to rounding in authorization buffers is just one example of how causal inference can improve customer experiences and generate significant business impact. Discontinuous thresholds naturally appear throughout consumer-facing products beyond payment systems and provide an opportunity to estimate (local) treatment effects without having to first run A/B tests. Other areas where regression discontinuity could be applied include relevance thresholds in search or ads, targeting thresholds for incentives, or product availability thresholds. If there is a threshold that generates a discontinuous product experience, regression discontinuity can be applied!

    If you would like to learn more about our work, check out the intro to our team or our upcoming posts on projects we’ve worked on. You can follow tech-at-instacart to be notified as they are published.

    And a special shoutout goes to Aditya Karan, a former Instacart PhD Intern, who was instrumental in bringing this project to life!
